runch?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn47@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596108618668_ae8500a395ae4c74.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=JdixkwaHMQE:KxikrMezqtc:-BTjWOF_DHI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn91@2020_5/2020-07-30/1596108618845_3ad70c8f3c34fa1b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=JdixkwaHMQE:KxikrMezqtc:D7DqB2pKExk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn47@2020_3/2020-07-30/1596108617758_d098beb5832ca4cb.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn75@2020_3/2020-07-30/1596108619649_749e2568e12b832a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Techcrunch/~4/JdixkwaHMQE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn79@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596108618795_ebb70693733f2545.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-07-30 19:29:59","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 11:29:59","title":"GM starts construction on the cornerstone of its EV strategy","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2024160","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Steel construction has begun on the nearly 3-million-square-foot factory that will mass produce Ultium battery cells and packs, the cornerstone of General Motors&#8217; strategy to bring 20 electric vehicles to market by 2023.</p>\n<p>The Ultium Cells LLC battery cell manufacturing facility in Lordstown, Ohio is part of a joint venture between GM and LG Chem that was announced in December. At the time, the two companies committed to invest up $2.3 billion into the new joint venture as well as establish a battery cell assembly plant on a greenfield manufacturing site in the Lordstown area of Northeast Ohio that will create more than 1,100 new jobs. The factory will be able produce 30 gigawatts hours of capacity annually. To put that into perspective, Tesla&#8217;s factory in Sparks, Nevada, which is part of a partnership with Panasonic, has a 35 GW hour capacity.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2024218\" style=\"width: 610px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant205.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2024218\" class=\"wp-image-2024218 size-full\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant205.jpg\" alt=\"GM Ultium Cells factory\" width=\"600\" height=\"400\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant205.jpg 600w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant205.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant205.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant205.jpg?resize=50,33 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 600px) 100vw, 600px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2024218\" class=\"wp-caption-text\">Construction at the all-new Ultium Cells LLC battery cell manufacturing facility in Lordstown, Ohio.</p></div></p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>The batteries and an underlying electric architecture will be used in a broad range of products across its Cadillac, Buick, Chevrolet and GMC brands as well as the Cruise Origin autonomous shuttle that was revealed in January. The Cadillac Lyriq EV flagship and an all-electric GMC Hummer, which will be revealed this fall and go into production in the fourth quarter of 2021, will use the Ultium battery system. GM plans to reveal the Lyriq at a virtual event August 6.</p>\n<p>This modular architecture, called “Ultium,” (same as the battery) will be capable of 19 different battery and drive unit configurations, 400-volt and 800-volt packs with storage ranging from 50 kWh to 200 kWh, and front-, rear- and all-wheel drive configurations. At the heart of the new modular architecture, will be the large-format pouch battery cells manufactured this new factory.</p>\n<p>GM broke ground at the factory site in May and has since poured the concrete footings for the facility. Steel construction will continue into fall 2020, according to GM.</p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2024256\" style=\"width: 610px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant193.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2024256\" class=\"wp-image-2024256 size-full\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant193.jpg\" alt=\"GM Ultium Battery Cell Manufacturing Factory\" width=\"600\" height=\"400\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant193.jpg 600w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant193.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant193.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/UltiumLLCPlant193.jpg?resize=50,33 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 600px) 100vw, 600px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2024256\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> GM</p></div></p>\n<p>GM has used LG Chem as a lithium-ion and electronics supplier for at least a decade. The companies began working together in 2009. The relationship deepened as GM developed and then launched the Chevy Bolt EV.</p>\n<p>This latest joint venture marks a step change for GM and a means to accelerating its EV plans, which could even involve <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/reuters/2020/07/29/business/29reuters-gm-results-evs.html\">spinning out the operations</a> into a separate company.</p>\n<p class=\"css-158dogj evys1bk0\">&#8220;We are open to looking at and evaluate anything that we think is going to drive long-term shareholder value, so I would say nothing is off the table,&#8221; GM Chairman and CEO Mary Barra said during an earnings call Wednesday.</p>\n<p>GM reported Wednesday a <a href=\"https://media.gm.com/content/dam/Media/gmcom/investor/2020/jul/gm-q2-2020-earnings-press-release.pdf\">$758 million loss in the second quarter</a> on $16.78 billion in revenue. The loss and the 53% year-over-year drop in revenue was caused mostly by COVID-19-related factory shutdowns in the U.S. However, the results still managed to beat analysts expectations.</p>\n<div 19:29:59","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 11:29:59","title":"The Hummer EV is shaping up to be GM’s electric answer to the Ford Bronco and Tesla Cybertruck","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2024052","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">The video below contains the first glimpse at the upcoming electric GMC Hummer. The preview video is short, full of nonsense buzzwords, but still telling. It’s clear GM identified two main competitors against the upcoming Hummer: The <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/07/13/ford-blends-tech-and-nostalgia-in-the-2021-bronco/\">Ford Bronco</a> and <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/tag/tesla-cybertruck/\">Tesla Cybertruck</a>.</p>\n<p>The Hummer EV was announced pre-COVID 19 during the Super Bowl. At the time, GM promised it would feature 1,000 HP from the electric powertrain. Since then, little has been released about the upcoming vehicle, though GM maintains it’s still on track for production in the fall of 2021.</p>\n<p>The video released today sports a handful of expected features and capabilities. Interestingly enough, these features are on both sides of the motoring spectrum. If categorized, they fall into two groups: on-road thrills and off-roading adventure. The video paints a clear picture: GM is targeting the Hummer EV against the <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/tesla-motors\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"tesla-motors\">Tesla <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> Cybertruck and the Ford Bronco — both vehicles that are getting a lot of attention because of their capabilities and design.</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>For positioning against the Cybertruck, GM is touting the Hummer EV’s power of 1,000 HP and 11,500 lb-ft of torque (though this number is derived in a different fashion than usual). It’s also saying the massive truck can hit 60 mph in 3 seconds, which is in the same realm as the top sports cars. Lastly, the video teaser stated the Hummer EV has an Adrenaline Mode, which is easy to assume is similar to Tesla’s Ludicrous mode, along with improved self-driving capability.</p>\n<p>For the Bronco, GM is showing the Hummer EV’s off-roading features, including a so-called Open Air Infinity Roof and Modular Sky Panels, which is likely similar to the Bronco’s expansive removable roof. Even more telling is the Crab Mode mentioned in the video. Crab Mode is likely a high-torque rock crawling mode for when bouldering off-road. With the crazy amount of torque available, the Hummer EV will probably be able to crawl up impressive inclines.</p>\n<p>Pricing and exact availability have yet to be announced, and the same can be said about the Tesla Cybertruck. And don’t forget about the upcoming electric Ford F-150. There’s a war of the electric pickup coming, and I’m here for it.</p>\n<p><iframe class='youtube-player' width='640' height='360' 19:29:59","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 11:29:59","title":"Zuckerberg unconvincingly feigns ignorance of data-sucking VPN scandal","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2024285","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Facebook&#8217;s <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/person/mark-zuckerberg\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"person\" data-entity=\"mark-zuckerberg\">Mark Zuckerberg <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> appeared less than entirely truthful at today&#8217;s House Judiciary hearing, regarding last year&#8217;s major Onavo controversy, in which his company paid teenagers to use a VPN app that reported detailed data on their internet use. Though he may not have outright lied about it, his answers were evasive and misleading enough to warrant a rushed clarification shortly afterward.</p>\n<p>Rep. Hank Johnson (D-GA) was asking Zuckerberg to confirm a series events last year first reported by TechCrunch: A VPN app called Onavo, owned by Facebook, was <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2018/08/22/apple-facebook-onavo/\">kicked out of Apple&#8217;s App Store</a> for collecting and reporting usage data while purporting to provide a protective service.</p>\n<p>Soon afterward, Facebook quietly <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/01/29/facebook-project-atlas/\">began paying people — 18 percent of whom were teenagers — to install the &#8220;Facebook Research&#8221; app</a>, which did much the same thing as Onavo under a different name. TechCrunch reported this and <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/01/30/apple-bans-facebook-vpn/\">Apple issued a ban</a> before the end of that day; Facebook claimed to have removed it voluntarily, but this was shown not to be true.</p>\n<p>Rep. Johnson questioned Zuckerberg along these lines, and the latter repeatedly expressed his unsureness about and lack of familiarity with these issues.</p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>Johnson</strong>: When it became public that Facebook was using Onavo to conduct digital surveillance, your company got kicked out of Apple&#8217;s App store, isn&#8217;t that true?</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, I&#8217;m not sure I&#8217;d characterize it in that way.</p>\n<p><strong>Johnson</strong>: I mean, Onavo did get kicked out of the app store, isn&#8217;t that true?</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, we took the app out after Apple changed their policies on VPN apps.</p>\n<p><strong>Johnson</strong>: And it was because of the use of the surveillance tools.</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, I&#8217;m not sure the policy was worded that way or that it&#8217;s exactly the right characterization of it&#8230; [The policies are explained below.]</p>\n<p><strong>Johnson</strong>: Let me ask you this question, after Onavo was booted out of the app store, you turned to other surveillance tools, such as Facebook Research App, correct?</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, in general, yes, we do a broad variety—</p>\n<p><strong>Johnson</strong>: Isn&#8217;t it true, Mr. Zuckerberg, that Facebook paid teenagers to sell their privacy by installing Facebook Research App?</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, I&#8217;m not familiar with that, but I think it&#8217;s a general practice that companies use to, uh, have different surveys and understand data from how people are using different products and what their preferences are.</p>\n<p><strong>Johnson</strong>: Facebook Research app got thrown out of the App Store too, isn&#8217;t that true?</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, I&#8217;m not familiar with that.</p></blockquote>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2024315\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/rep-johnson.jpg 1494w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/rep-johnson.jpg?resize=150,89 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/rep-johnson.jpg?resize=680,405 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/rep-johnson.jpg?resize=50,30 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2024315\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:</strong> YouTube</p></div></p>\n<p>Of course, the idea that Zuckerberg was not familiar with events that made headlines, took down Facebook&#8217;s internal apps for days, and prompted an angry letter to him from a senator <a href=\"https://twitter.com/JoshConstine/status/1288595894435106817\">is absurd</a>. (After all, Facebook <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/02/28/facebook-research-teens/\">responded</a>.)</p>\n<p>Perhaps intuiting that this particular claim of ignorance was a bridge too far (and perhaps in response to some frantic off-screen action in the CEO&#8217;s barnlike virtual testimony HQ), Zuckerberg took the opportunity to backpedal a few minutes later:</p>\n<blockquote><p>In response to Congressman Johnson’s question, before I said that I wasn’t familiar with the Facebook research app when I wasn’t familiar with that name for it. I just want to be clear that I do recall we used an app for research and it’s since been discontinued.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Of course, although Zuckerberg may plausibly have been unsure about the name, it&#8217;s not to be believed that he was not familiar with the events of that time, as they were both highly publicized and very costly for Facebook. Naturally he would also have been refreshed on them during preparation for this testimony.</p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"yAv9cnRVkC\"><p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/01/30/facebook-research-app/\">Senator Warner calls on Zuckerberg to support market research consent rules</a></p></blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" title=\"&#8220;Senator Warner calls on Zuckerberg to support market research consent rules&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/01/30/facebook-research-app/embed/#?secret=yAv9cnRVkC\" data-secret=\"yAv9cnRVkC\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe></div>\n<p>That Zuckerberg is unfamiliar with the exact wording of Apple&#8217;s rules is possible, even probable, but it was no secret that the rules were changed basically in response to reports of Facebook&#8217;s Onavo shenanigans. Here is what Apple said at the time:</p>\n<blockquote><p>We work hard to protect user privacy and data security throughout the Apple ecosystem. With the latest update to our guidelines, we made it explicitly clear that apps should not collect information about which other apps are installed on a user’s device for the purposes of analytics or advertising/marketing and must make it clear what user data will be collected and how it will be used.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Later, when TechCrunch showed that Facebook had been using an enterprise deployment tool to essentially sideload spyware onto teenagers&#8217; phones, Apple said this:</p>\n<blockquote><p>We designed our Enterprise Developer Program solely for the internal distribution of apps within an organization. Facebook has been using their membership to distribute a data-collecting app to consumers, which is a clear breach of their agreement with Apple. Any developer using their enterprise certificates to distribute apps to consumers will have their certificates revoked, which is what we did in this case to protect our users and their data.</p></blockquote>\n<p>So Facebook was the reason, implicitly first, then later explicitly, for these App Store lockdowns. Rep. Johnson put the whole thing quite plainly at the end of his questions.</p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>Johnson</strong>: You tried one thing and then you got caught, made some apologies, then you did it all over again. [long pause]&#8230; Isn&#8217;t that true?</p>\n<p><strong>Zuckerberg</strong>: Congressman, I respectfully disagree with that characterization.</p></blockquote>\n<p>You can watch the full hearing here:</p>\n<p><iframe class='youtube-player' width='640' height='360' 19:29:59","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 11:29:59","title":"Connected audio was a bad choice","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2024142","description":"<p class=\"p3\">The past week, I’ve spent ample time looking to revamp my home audio setup. I think my only qualification is that my next setup is as dumb as possible.</p>\n<p id=\"speakable-summary\">In the past five years, my setup has gone from a fairly middling wired 2.1 speaker setup to a confusing menagerie of connected smart speakers. I&#8217;ve likely gone through at least five <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/google\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"google\">Google <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> Assistant-laden speakers including the Google Home Max, a couple connected <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/sonos\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"sonos\">Sonos <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> speakers, three HomePods, a Facebook Portal+, non-smart speakers connected via Chromecast Audio and god knows how many Alexa-integrated speakers. All in all, I can firmly say I have made some very bad audio decisions in my recent life.</p>\n<p>I&#8217;ve had a lot of frustrations with my current setup, but they&#8217;re really issues with the entire smart speaker market:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Good audio hardware should be timeless, and devices that need frequent firmware updates, have proprietary support for a certain operating system or can lose integration support quickly fly in the face of that.</li>\n<li>Home entertainment integrations with these speakers are just awful, even among products built by the same company. Repeatedly connecting my stereo HomePods to my Apple TV has been maddening.</li>\n<li>Smart assistants are much less ambitious than they were years ago and the ceiling of innovation already seems to have come down significantly. Third party integrations have sunk far below expectations and it&#8217;s pretty uncertain that these voice interfaces have as bright a future as these tech companies once hoped.</li>\n<li>These assistants were once going to be the operating systems of the home, but the smart home experiment largely feels like a failure and it&#8217;s growing clearer that the dream of a Jarvis-like system that plays nicely with all of your internet-connected devices was totally naive.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>All in all, it&#8217;s time for me to move on and invest some cash in a setup that will sound good for decades.</p>\n<p>Now, many of you will say that my true error was a lack of commitment to one ecosystem, which is undoubtedly spot-on and yet I don’t think any of the players had precisely what I wanted hence the wildly piecemeal approach. Dumping more funds into a robust Sonos setup probably would have been the wisest commitment, but I have commitment issues and I think part of it was a desire to see what was out there.</p>\n<p>In quarantine, I’ve gotten ample time to spend with my home audio system and the destructive weave on non-compatible hardware is all too much. I don&#8217;t want my speakers to have their own operating systems or for one speaker to play nice with my music streaming platform of choice, but not the other. I want something that can last.</p>\n<p>After doing half-commits to several ecosystems, I feel I&#8217;ve seen and heard it all and now I&#8217;m shopping for some good old-fashioned dumb wired surround sound speakers to integrate with a <em>slightly</em> smarter AV receiver. God willing, I will have strength to not buy whatever cool audio gadgets come out next year and can stay strong. If you have some good tips on a nice setup, please help me out.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?a=snAMqEjuYFA:14ycr5ZEGAM:2mJPEYqXBVI\"><img Matney","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=2mJPEYqXBVI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn72@2020_6/2020-07-30/1596108600778_cda007d67cf2c798.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=7Q72WNTAKBA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn59@2020_5/2020-07-30/1596108619261_6be3d6b3141e6636.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn47@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596108618668_ae8500a395ae4c74.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=snAMqEjuYFA:14ycr5ZEGAM:-BTjWOF_DHI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn27@2020_3/2020-07-30/1596108618837_8ea2cc482427c55a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=snAMqEjuYFA:14ycr5ZEGAM:D7DqB2pKExk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn87@2020_3/2020-07-30/1596108619781_1d097492be425ebe.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn75@2020_3/2020-07-30/1596108619649_749e2568e12b832a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Techcrunch/~4/snAMqEjuYFA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn11@2020_6/2020-07-30/1596108619465_19e0c031a4f956d2.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-07-30 19:29:59","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 11:29:59","title":"Apple CEO Tim Cook questioned over App Store’s removal of rival screen time apps in antitrust hearing","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2024242","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Last year, <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/apple\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"apple\">Apple<a <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2018/12/05/apple-puts-third-party-screen-time-apps-on-notice/\"> removed a number of screen time</a> and parental control apps from its App Store, shortly after the company had released its own first-party screen time solution with the <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2018/09/17/ios-12-is-now-available-to-download/\">launch</a> of iOS 12. At today&#8217;s antitrust hearing, Apple CEO Tim Cook was questioned about the move, given the anti-competitive implications.</p>\n<p>Shortly after Apple debuted its own Screen Time feature set, <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2018/12/05/apple-puts-third-party-screen-time-apps-on-notice/\">several third-party app makers suddenly saw</a> their own screen time solutions come under increased App Store review. Many apps also saw their app updates rejected or their apps removed entirely. The impacted developers had used a range of methods to track screen time, as there was no official means to do so. This had included the use of background location, VPNs, and MDM-based solutions, and sometimes a combination of methods.</p>\n<p>Apple defended its decision at the time, saying the removals had put users&#8217; privacy and security at risk, given that they required access to a device&#8217;s location, app use, email accounts, camera permissions, and more.</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>But lawmakers questioned Apple&#8217;s decision to suddenly seem to care about the user privacy threats coming from these apps &#8212; many of which had been on the market for years.</p>\n<p>Rep. Lucy McBath (GA-D) began the line of questioning by reading an email from a mother who wrote to Apple about her disappointment over the apps&#8217; removals, saying that Apple&#8217;s move was &#8220;reducing consumer access to much-needed services to keep children safe and protect their mental health and well-being.&#8221; She then asked why Apple had removed apps from competitors shortly after releasing its own screen time solution.</p>\n<p>Cook responded much as Apple did last year, by saying the company was concerned about the &#8220;privacy and security of kids,&#8221; and that the technology the apps used was problematic.</p>\n<p>&#8220;The technology that was being used at that time was called MDM, and it had the ability to sort of take over the kid&#8217;s screen, and a third party could could see it,&#8221; Cook said. &#8220;So we were worried about their safety.&#8221;</p>\n<p>That&#8217;s perhaps not the most accurate description of how MDM works, as it describes MDM as some sneaky remote control tool. In reality, MDM technology has legitimate uses in the mobile ecosystem and continues to be used today. However, it was designed for enterprise use &#8212; like managing a fleet of employee devices, for example, not consumer phones. MDM tools can access a device&#8217;s location, control app use, email, and set various permissions, among other things that a corporate entity may want to do as part of their efforts in securing employee devices.</p>\n<p>In a way, that&#8217;s why it made sense for parents who wanted to similarly control and lockdown their children&#8217;s iPhones. Though not a consumer technology, the app developers had seen a hole in the market and had found a way to fill it using the tools at their disposal. That&#8217;s how the market works.</p>\n<p>Apple&#8217;s argument, isn&#8217;t wrong, though. The way the apps used MDM was a privacy risk. But rather than banning the apps outright, it should have offered them an alternative. That is, instead of just booting out its competition, it should have also built a developer API for its iOS Screen Time solution in addition to the consumer-facing product.</p>\n<p>Such an API could have allowed developers to build apps that could tap into Apple&#8217;s own screen time features and parental controls. Apple could have given the apps a deadline to make the transition instead of ending their businesses. This wouldn&#8217;t have harmed the developers or their end users, and would have addressed the privacy concerns associated with the third-party apps.</p>\n<p>&#8220;The timing of the removals seem very coincidental,&#8221; McBath pointed out. &#8220;If Apple wasn&#8217;t attempting to harm competitors in order to help its own app, why did Phil Schiller, who runs the App Store, promote the Screen Time app to customers who complained about the removal of rival parental control apps?,&#8221; she asked.</p>\n<p>Cook replied that there are today over 30 screen time apps in the App Store so there is &#8220;vibrant competition for parental controls out there.&#8221;</p>\n<p>But McBath noted that some banned apps were allowed back into the App Store six months later, without any significant privacy changes. (Apple rolled out new <a href=\"https://developer.apple.com/app-store/review/guidelines/#mobile-device-management\">rules</a> for MDM apps in June 2019.)</p>\n<p>&#8220;Six month is truly an eternity for small businesses to be shut down. Even worse, if all the while a larger competitor is actually taking away customers,&#8221; she said.</p>\n<p>Tim Cook wasn&#8217;t given a chance to respond further to this line of questioning as the McBath moved on to question Apple&#8217;s refusal to allow Random House a way to sell e-books in its own app outside of Apple&#8217;s iBooks.</p>\n<p>Cook deflected that question, saying &#8220;there are many reasons why the app might not initially go through the App store,&#8221; noting it could have been a technical problem.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/tag/tech-antitrust-hearing\"><img src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/tech-antitrust-hearing-banner.jpg\" /></a></p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?a=gPvp_oaCQHs:L3RYEgNpGcM:2mJPEYqXBVI\"><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=2mJPEYqXBVI\" border=\"0\"></img></a> <a +0000","feedId":26,"bgimg":"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227829497.jpeg?w=600","linkMd5":"768adf2e1a82f023bf1c5568a11997c0","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn92@2020_2/2020-07-30/1596108617788_91f4943a46f03155.webp","author":"Sarah Perez","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/tech-antitrust-hearing-banner.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn64@2020_4/2020-07-30/1596108601225_b509d3f7f12e9f30.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=2mJPEYqXBVI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn72@2020_6/2020-07-30/1596108600778_cda007d67cf2c798.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=7Q72WNTAKBA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn59@2020_5/2020-07-30/1596108619261_6be3d6b3141e6636.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn47@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596108618668_ae8500a395ae4c74.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=gPvp_oaCQHs:L3RYEgNpGcM:-BTjWOF_DHI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn75@2020_4/2020-07-30/1596108617798_28dadb6c9b46ac89.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=gPvp_oaCQHs:L3RYEgNpGcM:D7DqB2pKExk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn75@2020_5/2020-07-30/1596108619532_d053a21f714a770e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn75@2020_3/2020-07-30/1596108619649_749e2568e12b832a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Techcrunch/~4/gPvp_oaCQHs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn79@2020_6/2020-07-30/1596108617621_3b6b1ff84b7e2b91.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-07-30 19:29:59","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 11:29:59","title":"Bezos ‘can’t guarantee’ no anti-competitive activity as Congress catches him flat-footed","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2024033","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">At today&#8217;s House Judiciary Committee hearing, <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/amazon\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"amazon\">Amazon <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> CEO Jeff Bezos bungled attempts to assuage concerns that the company poaches ideas from its competitors, taking sustained fire from Rep. Pramila Jayapal (D-WA) and others. Faced with testimony from employees that there is &#8220;nobody enforcing&#8221; policies, resulting in &#8220;a candy shop&#8221; of seller data, Bezos admitted to what amounts to ignorance or complicity.</p>\n<p>Jayapal&#8217;s interrogation was one of precious few substantive exchanges in a hearing dominated by tiresome grandstanding and uninformed or irrelevant lines of questions.</p>\n<p>Amazon has been dogged for years with well-substantiated allegations that it uses its bird&#8217;s-eye position in online retail, much of which passes through its platform, to spot new products and categories and enter them using that inside information — often taking huge losses to undercut the existing players in the market.</p>\n<p>Jayapal — who represents the district in which the company is headquartered and has gone head to head with the company numerous times — began by asking straight out whether Amazon does this (Wording of questions and answers has only been slightly edited for clarity.):</p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>Jayapal</strong>: Does Amazon have access and use third party seller data when making business decisions?</p>\n<p><strong>Bezos</strong>: I can&#8217;t answer that question yes or no. I can tell you we have a policy against using seller-specific data to aid our private label business but I can&#8217;t guarantee you that that policy has never been violated.</p></blockquote>\n<p>In response, Jayapal cited a <a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/amazon-scooped-up-data-from-its-own-sellers-to-launch-competing-products-11587650015\">recent WSJ report</a> citing multiple instances where Amazon had done just that:</p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>Jayapal</strong>: You&#8217;re probably aware that an April 2020 report in the Wall Street Journal revealed that your company does access data on third party sellers both by reviewing data on popular individual products and sellers and making tiny categories that allowed your company to categorically access detailed seller information in a supposedly aggregate category do you deny that report?</p>\n<p><strong>Bezos</strong>: I&#8217;m familiar with the Wall Street Journal article you&#8217;re talking about. We continue to look into that very carefully, I&#8217;m not yet satisfied we&#8217;ve gotten to the bottom of it. It&#8217;s not as easy to do it as you think because some of the sources in the article are anonymous.</p>\n<p><strong>Jayapal</strong>: I&#8217;ll take that as a you&#8217;re not denying that.</p></blockquote>\n<p>\t<div class=\"article-block block--pullout block--right\">\n\t\t<blockquote>\n\t\t\t&#8220;It&#8217;s a candy shop, everyone can have access to anything they want.&#8221;\t\t\t\t\t</blockquote>\n\t</div>\n\t Her next move was a damning quote from a former employee that Bezos seemed at a loss to counter.</p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>Jayapal</strong>: A former Amazon employee and third party sales an recruitment told this committee, quote, &#8220;there&#8217;s a rule but there&#8217;s nobody enforcing or spot checking. They just say don&#8217;t help yourself to the data. It&#8217;s a candy shop, everyone can have access to anything they want.&#8221; Do category managers have access to non public data about third party products and businesses?</p>\n<p><strong>Bezos</strong>: Uh, here&#8217;s what I can tell you. We do have certain safeguards in place. we train people on the policy, we expect people to follow that policy the same we would with any others. It&#8217;s a voluntary policy [in that having such a policy is voluntary, not that it is voluntary to follow that policy, he clarified]. If we found that someone violated it, we would take action against them.</p>\n<p><strong>Jayapal</strong>: Well, there&#8217;s numerous reports and the committee has conducted interviews with former employees that confirm that there are employees that do have access to that data and are using it. If you thought you were actually enforcing these rules, do you think that that&#8217;s working? There&#8217;s credible reporting that has documented breaches of these rules you&#8217;ve put into place, and this committee has interviewed employees that say that these breaches typically occur.</p></blockquote>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2024132\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227830755.jpeg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2024132\" class=\"wp-image-2024132 size-full\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227830755.jpeg\" alt=\"WASHINGTON, DC - JULY 29: Rep. Pramila Jayapal, D-WA, speaks during the House Judiciary Subcommittee hearing on Antitrust, Commercial and Administrative Law on Online Platforms and Market Power in the Rayburn House office Building, July 29, 2020 on Capitol Hill in Washington, DC.\" width=\"1024\" height=\"660\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227830755.jpeg 1024w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227830755.jpeg?resize=150,97 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227830755.jpeg?resize=300,193 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227830755.jpeg?resize=768,495 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227830755.jpeg?resize=680,438 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227830755.jpeg?resize=50,32 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2024132\" class=\"wp-caption-text\">(Photo by Mandel Ngan-Pool/Getty Images)</p></div></p>\n<p>After confirming that &#8220;aggregate&#8221; data could be on as little as a single seller under Amazon&#8217;s policy, she then began a lengthy presentation of why this was obviously a serious concern for competition:</p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>Jayapal</strong>: Interviews with former employees have made it clear that that aggregate data essentially allows access to highly detailed data in those product categories &#8211; there&#8217;s the example of Fortem, a small business that had no direct competitors, except for Amazon Warehouse Deals, a resale clearance account that only sold 17 units. An Amazon employee accessed a detailed sales report on Fortem&#8217;s product with information on how much the company spent on advertising per unit and the cost to ship each trunk. And then Amazon launched its own competing products in October 2019. That&#8217;s a major loophole. And I go back to the general counsel&#8217;s statement to this committee very clearly that there was no access to this data, that Amazon does not use that data for its own benefit. And I&#8217;m now hearing you say, well you&#8217;re not so sure that&#8217;s going on.</p>\n<p><strong>&#8230;</strong>You have access to the entirety of sellers pricing and inventory information, past, present and future, and you dictate the participation of third party sellers on your platform. So you can set the rules of the game for your competitors but not actually follow those rules yourself. Do you think that&#8217;s fair to the mom and pop third party bus that are trying to sell on your platform?</p>\n<p><strong>Bezos</strong>: I appreciate that question and I like it a lot, because I really wanted a chance to address that. I&#8217;m very proud of what we&#8217;ve done for third party sellers on this platform&#8230;</p></blockquote>\n<p>Sensing Bezos was bloviating and/or playing for time, Jayapal cut him off to make her final point:</p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>Jayapal</strong>: So you might allow third party sellers onto your platform, but if you&#8217;re continuously monitoring the data to make sure that they&#8217;re never gonna get big enough that they can compete with you, that is actually the concern that the committee has. The whole goal of this committee&#8217;s work is to make sure there are more Amazons, more Apples, more companies that get to innovate and small businesses that get to thrive. That is why we need to regulate these marketplaces, so that no company has a platform so dominant that it is essentially a monopoly.</p></blockquote>\n<p>\t<div class=\"article-block block--pullout block--right\">\n\t\t<blockquote>\n\t\t\t&#8220;Mr. Bezos, did you personally sign off on the plan to raise prices after Amazon eliminated its competition?&#8221; &#8220;I don&#8217;t remember that at all.&#8221;\t\t\t\t\t</blockquote>\n\t</div>\n\tRep. Jayapal&#8217;s questions seemed to open the season on Bezos. Representative Mary Scanlon (D-PA) soon highlighted Amazon&#8217;s willingness to lose hundreds of millions of dollars to kneecap the competing Diapers.com:</p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>Scanlon</strong>: The price war against Diapers.com worked, and within a few months it was struggling, and so Amazon bought it. After buying your leading competitor here, Amazon cut promotions like Amazon.mom and the steep discounts it used to lure customers away from Diapers.com, and then increase the prices of diapers for new moms and dads. Mr. Bezos, did you personally sign off on the plan to raise prices after Amazon eliminated its competition?</p>\n<p><strong>Bezos</strong>: I don&#8217;t remember that at all.  What I remember is that we matched competitors&#8217; prices and I believe we followed diapers.com. This was 11 years ago so you&#8217;re asking a lot of my memory.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Then the Chairman, Rep. David Ciccilline (D-RI), took the opportunity to get some more direct answers on Rep. Jayapal&#8217;s line of questions.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227828907.jpeg\"><img class=\"aligncenter wp-image-2024056 size-full\" src=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227828907.jpeg\" alt=\"House Judiciary Subcommittee on Antitrust, Commercial and Administrative Law Chair David Cicilline, D-RI, speaks during a hearing on &#34;Online Platforms and Market Power&#34; in the Rayburn House office Building on Capitol Hill in Washington, DC on July 29, 2020. (Photo by MANDEL NGAN/POOL/AFP via Getty Images)\" width=\"1024\" height=\"646\" srcset=\"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227828907.jpeg 1024w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227828907.jpeg?resize=150,95 150w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227828907.jpeg?resize=300,189 300w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227828907.jpeg?resize=768,485 768w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227828907.jpeg?resize=680,429 680w, https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227828907.jpeg?resize=50,32 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" /></a></p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>Cicilline</strong>: Isn&#8217;t it an inherent conflict of interest for Amazon to produce and sell products on its platform that compete directly with third party sellers, particularly when you, Amazon, set the rules of the game?</p>\n<p><strong>Bezos</strong>: Thank you&#8230; no, I don&#8217;t believe it is. We have&#8230; the consumer is ultimately the one making the decisions about what to buy, what price to buy it at, who to buy it from.</p>\n<p><strong>Cicilline</strong>: That&#8217;s not the question, Mr. Bezos. The question is is there an inherent conflict of interest. &#8230;You said you can&#8217;t guarantee that the policy of not sharing third party sellers data with Amazon&#8217;s own line hasn&#8217;t been violated, you can&#8217;t be certain. Can you please explain that to me? Can you list examples of when that policy has been violated? Shouldn&#8217;t third parties know for sure that data isn&#8217;t being shared with your own line of competitors?</p>\n<p><strong>Bezos</strong>: What think is important to understand is we have a policy against using the individual seller data to compete with our private label products.</p>\n<p><strong>Cicilline</strong>: You couldn&#8217;t assure Ms. Jayapal that that policy isn&#8217;t violated routinely!</p>\n<p><strong>Bezos</strong>: Well, I mean, we are investigating that. I do not want to go beyond what I know right now, but we are as a result of that Wall Street Journal article, we are looking at that very carefully.</p></blockquote>\n<p>The Committee&#8217;s questioning of Google&#8217;s Sundar Pichai, Facebook&#8217;s Mark Zuckerberg, and Apple&#8217;s Tim Cook was nowhere near this level, but the Amazon portions were effective — if only to see one of the world&#8217;s richest and most powerful people stumbling over his words, unable or unwilling to answer basic questions. Bezos has little experience with these hearings and it showed — no doubt this has been as informative for him as it has been for Congress.</p>\n<p><iframe class='youtube-player' width='640' src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Techcrunch/~4/Emao7BE6AQw\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 29 Jul 2020 20:52:50 +0000","feedId":26,"bgimg":"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227830755.jpeg","linkMd5":"94a26f9e0c681dad07e8256d0df8373b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn68@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596108601302_0754e63a1ce05755.webp","author":"Devin Coldewey","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227830755.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn68@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596108601302_0754e63a1ce05755.webp","https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/GettyImages-1227828907.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn31@2020_6/2020-07-30/1596108617995_2ddc132a43ee0955.webp","https://techcrunch.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/07/tech-antitrust-hearing-banner.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn64@2020_4/2020-07-30/1596108601225_b509d3f7f12e9f30.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=2mJPEYqXBVI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn72@2020_6/2020-07-30/1596108600778_cda007d67cf2c798.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=7Q72WNTAKBA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn59@2020_5/2020-07-30/1596108619261_6be3d6b3141e6636.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn47@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596108618668_ae8500a395ae4c74.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=Emao7BE6AQw:s3pVOeS5mxc:-BTjWOF_DHI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn83@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596108619699_14eb20ae078b4b9e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?i=Emao7BE6AQw:s3pVOeS5mxc:D7DqB2pKExk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn31@2020_3/2020-07-30/1596108619025_c3e01d29ac2450df.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/Techcrunch?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn75@2020_3/2020-07-30/1596108619649_749e2568e12b832a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/Techcrunch/~4/Emao7BE6AQw":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn51@2020_3/2020-07-30/1596108619074_35a00a7330a20f56.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-07-30 19:29:59","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 11:29:59","title":"Google’s ‘no choice’ screen on Android isn’t working, says Ecosia — querying the EU’s approach to antitrust enforcement","link":"https://techcrunch.com/?p=2023792","description":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\"><a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/google\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"google\">Google <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> alternative <a href=\"https://www.ecosia.org/?c=en\">Ecosia</a> is on a mission to turn search clicks into trees. The Berlin based not-for-profit reached a major milestone earlier this month, having used ad revenue generated by users of its <a href=\"https://info.ecosia.org/privacy\">privacy-sensitive</a> search engine to plant more than 100 million trees across 25 countries worldwide &#8212; targeted at biodiversity hotspots.</p>\n<p>However these good feels have been hit hard by the coronavirus pandemic. <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/ecosia\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"ecosia\">Ecosia <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> has seen its monthly revenues slashed by half since COVID-19 arrived in Europe, with turnover falling from €2.6M in February to just €1.4M in June. It&#8217;s worried that its promise of planting a tree every 0.8 seconds is at risk.</p>\n<p>It has also suffered a knock to regional visibility as a result of boycotting an auction process that <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/android\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"android\">Android <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> OS maker Google has been running throughout this year, as a response to a 2018 Commission antitrust decision that found the tech giant had violated EU competition rules in how it operates the smartphone platform &#8212; including via conditions placed on phone makers to pre-load its own services (like Google search) as device defaults.</p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"></div>\n<p>An auction process now determines which rival search engines appear on <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/08/02/google-to-auction-slots-on-android-default-search-choice-screen-in-europe-next-year-rivals-cry-pay-to-play-foul/\">a search &#8216;choice screen&#8217; Google began showing to Android users in Europe</a> in the wake of the Commission decision. Currently, Google offers three paid slots via the auction to non-Google search engines. Android users setting up a new device always see Google&#8217;s own search engine as one of the four total options.</p>\n<p>The tech giant&#8217;s rivals have consistently argued this <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/08/02/google-to-auction-slots-on-android-default-search-choice-screen-in-europe-next-year-rivals-cry-pay-to-play-foul/\">&#8216;pay to play&#8217; model is no remedy</a> for its anti-competitive behavior with Android, the world&#8217;s dominant smartphone OS. Although most (including <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/01/10/duckduckgo-still-critical-of-googles-eu-android-choice-screen-auction-after-wining-a-universal-slot/\">DuckDuckGo</a>) felt forced to participate in its auction process from the get-go. Forgoing the most prominent route to the Android search market isn&#8217;t exactly a luxury most businesses could afford.</p>\n<p>Ecosia, a not-for-profit, was the last major hold out. But now it says it&#8217;s been forced <span style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\">to end its boycott in a bid to remain competitive in the region. This means it will</span> participate in the next auction round for the Android choice screen &#8212; scheduled for the beginning of Q4. If it wins any per country slots it will appear as a search choice option to those Android users in future, though likely not til next year given the length of the auction process.</p>\n<p>It remains highly critical of Google&#8217;s pay-to-play model, arguing it&#8217;s no remedy for the antitrust violations identified by the Commission. It also laments that EU lawmakers are taking a &#8216;wait and see&#8217; approach to determining whether Google&#8217;s &#8216;remedy&#8217; is actually restoring competition, given all the evidence to the contrary.</p>\n<p>&#8220;The main reason why we boycotted the auction is because we think it&#8217;s highly unfair and anticompetitive,&#8221; says Ecosia CEO Christian Kroll, speaking to TechCrunch via video chat. &#8220;Not only do we think that fair competition shouldn&#8217;t be sold off in an auction but also the way the auction is designed basically makes sure that only the least interesting options can win.</p>\n<p>&#8220;Since we have a business model where we use most of our revenues to plant trees we basically can&#8217;t really win in an auction model. If you&#8217;re already a search engine that&#8217;s quite well known&#8230; then you have a lot of cannibalization effects through this screen. So we&#8217;re basically paying for traffic that we would get for free anyway&#8230; So it&#8217;s just super unfair and anticompetitive.&#8221;</p>\n<p>Kroll expresses emphatic surprise that the Commission didn&#8217;t immediately reject Google&#8217;s auction model for the choice screen &#8212; saying it seems as if they&#8217;ve learned nothing from the EU&#8217;s <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2009/12/16/eu-microsoft-antitrust/\">earlier intervention</a> against Microsoft&#8217;s tying of its Internet Explorer browser with its dominant desktop OS, Windows. (In that case the saga ended after <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/microsoft\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"microsoft\">Microsoft <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> agreed to implement a ballot screen offering a choice of up to 12 browsers, which paved the road for Google to later gain share with its own Chrome browser.)</p>\n<p>For a brief initial period last year Google did offer a fee-less choice screen in Europe, pushing this out to existing Android devices &#8212; with search rivals selected <a href=\"https://www.blog.google/around-the-globe/google-europe/presenting-search-app-and-browser-options-android-users-europe/\">based on their market popularity per country</a> (which, in some markets, included Ecosia).</p>\n<p>However the tech giant said then that it would be &#8220;evolving&#8221; its implementation over time. And a <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/08/02/google-to-auction-slots-on-android-default-search-choice-screen-in-europe-next-year-rivals-cry-pay-to-play-foul/\">few months later</a> an auction model was announced as incoming for new Android devices &#8212; with that &#8216;pay-to-play&#8217; approach kicking off at the start of this year.</p>\n<p>Search rivals including <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/duck-duck-go\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"duck-duck-go\">DuckDuckGo <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> and Qwant <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/08/02/google-to-auction-slots-on-android-default-search-choice-screen-in-europe-next-year-rivals-cry-pay-to-play-foul/\">immediately cried foul</a>. Yet the response from the Commission has been to kick the can &#8212; with regulators offering platitudes that said they would &#8220;closely monitor&#8221;. They also claimed to be &#8220;committed to a full and effective implementation of the decision&#8221;.</p>\n<p>However the missing adjective in that statement is &#8216;fast&#8217;. <span style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\">Google rivals would argue that for a remedy to be effective it needs to happen really fast, like </span><em style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\">now</em><span style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\"> &#8212; or, for some of them, the risk really is going out of business. After all, the Commission&#8217;s Android antitrust decision (which, yes, </span><a style=\"background-color: #ffffff; font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\" href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2018/10/10/google-files-appeal-against-europes-5bn-antitrust-fine-for-android/\">Google is appealing</a>)<span style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\"> already </span><a style=\"background-color: #ffffff; font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\" href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2018/07/18/google-gets-slapped-5bn-by-eu-for-android-antitrust-abuse/\">dates back two full years</a><span style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\">. </span></p>\n<p>&#8220;I find it very surprising that the <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/european-commission\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"european-commission\">European Commission <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> hasn&#8217;t rejected [Google&#8217;s auction model] from the start because some of the key principles from what made the choice screen successful in the Microsoft case have just been completely disregarded and been turned around by Google to turn the whole concept of a choice screen to their advantage,&#8221; says Kroll. &#8220;We&#8217;re not even calling it the &#8216;choice screen&#8217; internally, we just call it the &#8216;auction screen&#8217;. And since we&#8217;re now stopping to boycott we call it the &#8216;no choice screen&#8217;.&#8221;</p>\n<p>&#8220;It&#8217;s Google&#8217;s way to give the impression that there&#8217;s free choice but there is no free choice,&#8221; he adds. &#8220;If Google&#8217;s objective here would be to create choice for the user then they would present the most interesting options, which are the search engines with the highest marketshares &#8212; so definitely us, DuckDuckGo and maybe some other players as well. But that&#8217;s not what they&#8217;re trying to do.&#8221;</p>\n<p>Kroll points out that another German search rival to Google, Cliqz, had to pull the plug on its anti-tracking alternative at the <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/05/01/cliqz-pulls-the-plug-on-a-european-anti-tracking-alternative-to-google-search/\">start of this year</a> &#8212; meaning there&#8217;s now one less homegrown anti-tracking rival to Google in play. And while Ecosia feels it has no choice but to participate in Google&#8217;s auction game Kroll says it also can&#8217;t know whether or not participating will result in Ecosia overpaying Google for leads that then mean it generates less revenue and can&#8217;t plant as many trees&#8230; Or, well, any trees if the worst were to happen.</p>\n<p>(NB: Kroll was speaking to TechCrunch ahead of signing an NDA that Google requires participants of the auction to sign which puts a legal limit on what they can say about the process once they&#8217;re involved &#8212; which, in turn, is a problematic element that another European search rival, Qwant, has also <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/08/02/google-to-auction-slots-on-android-default-search-choice-screen-in-europe-next-year-rivals-cry-pay-to-play-foul/\">complained is unfair</a>&#8230; )</p>\n<p>&#8220;We don&#8217;t have any choice left, other than to participate,&#8221; adds Kroll. &#8220;Because we want to have access to the Android platform. So basically Google has successfully bullied everyone to play to its own rules &#8212; and it&#8217;s a game where Google is not only the referee but also they get a free ticket and they are also players&#8230;</p>\n<p>&#8220;Somehow Google magically convinced the public but I think also the European Commission that they need to generate revenue in an auction because they have so many costs through the Android development and so on. It is of course true that they have costs&#8230; but they are also generating massive profit through the deals that they then make with the device makers and those profits are not at all shared.&#8221;</p>\n<p>Kroll points out that Google shells out a (<a href=\"https://www.onlineadtech.com/google-pay-apple-12bn-remain-safaris-default-search-engine/\">reported</a>) $12BN per year to be the default search engine in Safari on Apple&#8217;s iOS platform &#8212; even as it pays nothing to get in front of the vast majority of mobile searchers&#8217; eyeballs via Android (and does the same with Chrome).</p>\n<p>&#8220;If they would pay the same amount of money for those platform they would soon be bankrupt,&#8221; he argues. &#8220;So they are getting all this for free and they are also getting other benefits for free &#8212; like having the Play Store preinstalled, like having Google Maps preinstalled, YouTube preinstalled and so on &#8212; which are all revenue sources. But they&#8217;re not sharing any of those revenue. They just try to outsource all of the costs that they have to their competitors, which is I think very unfair.&#8221;</p>\n<p>While <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/organization/alphabet\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"organization\" data-entity=\"alphabet\">Alphabet, <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> Google&#8217;s parent entity, doesn&#8217;t break out Google Play revenue specifically from within a generic &#8220;advertising&#8221; bucket when it reports its financials, data from <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/06/30/global-app-revenue-jumps-to-50b-in-the-first-half-of-2020-in-part-due-to-covid-19-impacts/\">SensorTower </a>for the first half of 2020 suggests it generated $17.3BN in Play Store revenue alone over this six-month period, up 21% year-over-year. And Play is just one of the moneyspinners Google derives via &#8216;free&#8217; Android.</p>\n<p>Since the Commission&#8217;s antitrust 2018 decision against Android Kroll argues that nothing has changed for search competitors like Ecosia which are trying to offer consumers a more interesting value exchange for their clicks.</p>\n<p>&#8220;What Google is doing very successfully is they&#8217;re just playing on time,&#8221; he suggests. &#8220;Our competitor, Cliqz, already went bankrupt because of that. So the strategy seems to work really well for Google. And we also can&#8217;t afford to lose access to those platforms&#8230; I really hope that the European Commission will actually do something about this because it has been done successfully in the Microsoft case and we just need exactly the same.&#8221;</p>\n<p>Kroll also flags DuckDuckGo&#8217;s design suggestions for &#8220;a fair choice screen&#8221; &#8212; which <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/10/30/us-search-market-needs-a-choice-screen-remedy-now-says-duckduckgo/\">we covered here last year </a>but which Google (and the Commission) have so far simply ignored.</p>\n<p>He suspects regulators are waiting to see how the market looks in another year or more. But of course by then it may be too late to save more alternative search engines from a Cliqz-style demise, thereby further strengthening Google&#8217;s position. Which would obviously be the opposite of an antitrust remedy.</p>\n<p>Commissioner <a class=\"crunchbase-link\" href=\"https://crunchbase.com/person/margrethe-vestager\" target=\"_blank\" data-type=\"person\" data-entity=\"margrethe-vestager\">Margrethe Vestager <span class=\"crunchbase-tooltip-indicator\"></span></a> already conceded <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/10/08/europes-recharged-antitrust-chief-makes-her-five-year-pitch-to-be-digital-evp/\">last year</a> that another of her interventions against the tech giant &#8212; the <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/03/20/google-fined-1-49bn-in-europe-for-antitrust-violations-in-search-ad-brokering/\">Google AdSense antitrust case</a> &#8212; is an example of &#8220;enforcement that hasn’t succeeded because it has failed to restore competition&#8221;. So if she&#8217;s not careful her record on failed remedies could dent her high profile reputation for being an antitrust chief who&#8217;s at least willing to take on tech giants. Where competition is concerned, it must be all about outcomes &#8212; or what are you even doing as claimed law &#8216;enforcers&#8217;?</p>\n<p>&#8220;I always fear that the point might come when big corporates are more powerful than our public institutions and I&#8217;m wondering if this point isn&#8217;t already reached,&#8221; adds Kroll, positing that it&#8217;s not clear whether the EU &#8212; as an economic and political project now facing plenty of its own issues &#8212; will have enough resilience to be able to enforce its own competition law in the near future. So really his key point is: If not now, when? (Or, well, how?)</p>\n<p>It&#8217;s certainly true that there&#8217;s a growing disconnect between what the Commission is <em>saying</em> around competition policy and digital markets &#8212; where it&#8217;s alive to the critique that regulatory interventions need to be able to move much faster if they&#8217;re to prevent monopoly power irreversibly tipping these markets (it&#8217;s <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/06/02/europe-asks-for-views-on-platform-governance-and-competition-tools/\">currently consulting</a> on whether to give itself greater powers of intervention) &#8212; and its hands-off approach to how to remedy market failure. tl;dr there&#8217;s no effective enforcement without effective remedies. So dropping the ball after the fact of a decision really defeats the whole operation.</p>\n<p>Vestager clearly recognizes there&#8217;s a problem in the digital context &#8212; telling the EU parliament last year: &#8220;We have to consider remedies that are much more far reaching&#8221;. (Albeit, still not <em>committing</em> to having much more far reaching remedies.) Yet in parallel she <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/10/08/europes-recharged-antitrust-chief-makes-her-five-year-pitch-to-be-digital-evp/\">preaches</a> &#8216;wait and see&#8217; as her overarching philosophy &#8212; a policy &#8216;push-pull&#8217; which seems to be preventing the unit from even entertaining taking on a more agile, active and iterative role in supporting markets towards actual restoration of competition. At least not before a lengthy consultation exercise which further kicks the can,</p>\n<p>If EU lawmakers can&#8217;t learn the lessons from their own relatively recent digital antitrust history (Microsoft tying IE to Windows) to effectively enforce what is a pretty straightforwardly similar antitrust case (Google tying search &#38; its other services to Android), you have to question why they think they need new antitrust tools to properly tackle digital monopolies now. Given they don&#8217;t seem able to effectively wield the tools they&#8217;ve already got.</p>\n<p>It does rather look increasingly like the current crop of EU regulators have lost conviction &#8212; and/or fallen prey to risk aversion &#8212; in the face of platform power moves. (To wit: There are whispers the Commission is preparing to wave through <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2019/11/01/google-is-acquiring-fitbit/\">Google&#8217;s acquisition of Fitbit</a>, on <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/07/10/googles-fitbit-deal-could-avoid-eu-antitrust-probe-by-agreeing-not-to-use-health-data-for-ads/\">paper-thin promises from Google</a>, despite <a href=\"https://www.politico.eu/article/europe-must-not-rush-google-fitbit-deal-data-privacy/\">major concerns</a> raised about privacy and increased data consolidation &#8212; which, if true, would again mean the Commission <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2017/05/18/facebook-fined-122m-in-europe-over-misleading-whatsapp-filing/\">ignoring its own recent history of naively swallowing other similar tech giant claims</a>.)</p>\n<p>&#8220;My feeling is, what has happened in the Microsoft case&#8230; there was just somebody in the Commission crazy enough to say this is what the decision is and you have to do it&#8230; And maybe it just takes those kind of guts. That&#8217;s then maybe a political question. Is Vestager willing to really pick those battles?&#8221; asks Kroll.</p>\n<p>&#8220;My feeling is if people really understand the situation then they would care but you actually need to do a little bit of explaining that it&#8217;s not good to have a dominant player that is in such an important sector like search, and that is basically shutting down the market for everybody else.&#8221;</p>\n<p>Asked what his message is for the <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/07/28/how-to-watch-tech-hearing-antitrust/\">US lawmakers</a> now actively eyeing antitrust concerns around Google &#8212; and indeed <a href=\"https://techcrunch.com/2020/07/27/tech-hearing-apple-google-amazon-facebook-antitrust-house-judiciary/\">much of big tech</a> &#8212; Kroll says: &#8220;I&#8217;m a fan of competition and I also admire Google; I think Google is a very clever company but I think there is a point reached where there&#8217;s so much concentration of power that it gets dangerous for society&#8230; <span style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\">We&#8217;ve been suffering quite a lot from all the dominance that Google has in the various sectors. There are just things that Google are doing that are obviously anticompetitive.&#8221;</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\">One specific thing he suggests regulators take a close look at is how much money Google pays Apple to be the default search option on Safari. </span><span style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\">&#8220;It&#8217;s paying more money than it can actually afford to win the Safari search volume &#8212; that I think is very anticompetitive,&#8221; he argues. &#8220;They already own two-thirds of the market and they basically buy whatever&#8217;s left over so that they can just cement their dominance. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\">&#8220;The regulators should have a very close look at that and disallow Google to participate in any of those bids for default positions in other browsers in the future. I think that would even be beneficial for browsers because in the long term there would finally be competition for those spots again. Currently Google&#8217;s just winning them because they&#8217;re running out of options and there are not many other search providers left to choose from.&#8221;</span></p>\n<p>He also argues they need to make Google repair &#8220;<span style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\">some of the damage they&#8217;ve done&#8221; &#8212; i.e. as a result of unfairly gaining marketshare &#8212; by enforcing what he calls &#8220;a really fair choice screen&#8221;; non-paid and based on relevance for users. And by doing so on Android and Chrome devices. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\"> &#8220;I think until a year ago if you visited Google.com with your Safari browser or Firefox browser then Google would recommend to install Chrome. And for me that&#8217;s a clear abuse of one dominant position to support another part of your company,&#8221; he argues. &#8220;Google needs to repair that and that needs to happen very quickly &#8212; because otherwise other companies might [go out of business].&#8221;</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-size: 1.125rem; letter-spacing: -0.1px;\">&#8220;We&#8217;re still doing okay but we have been hit heavily by corona and we have a huge loss in revenue. Other companies might be hit even worse, I don&#8217;t know. And we don&#8217;t have the same deep pockets that the big players have. So other companies might disappear if nothing&#8217;s done soon,&#8221; he adds. </span></p>\n<p>We reached out to Google and the European Commission for comment.</p>\n<p>A Google spokesperson pointed us to its <a href=\"https://www.android.com/choicescreen/\">FAQ</a> about the auction. In further remarks which they specified could not be directly quoted they claimed an auction is a fair and objective method of determining how to fill available slots, adding that the revenue generated via the auction helps Google continue to invest in developing and maintaining Android.</p>\n<p>While a spokeswoman for the Commission told us it has been &#8220;discussing&#8221; the choice screen mechanism with Google, following what she described as &#8220;relevant feedback from the market, in particular in relation to the presentation and mechanics of the choice screen and to the selection mechanism of rival search providers&#8221;.</p>\n<p>The spokeswoman also reiterated earlier comments, that the Commission is continuing to monitor Google&#8217;s choice screen implementation and is &#8220;committed to a full and effective implementation of the decision&#8221;.</p>\n<p>However a source familiar with the matter said EU lawmakers view paid premium placement for a few cents as far superior to what Google was offering rivals before &#8212; i.e. no visibility at all &#8212; and thus take the view that that something is better than nothing.</p>\n<div
